<p>There are a number of surgical procedures performed on the circulatory system:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Coronary_artery_bypass_surgery" title="Coronary artery bypass surgery">Coronary artery bypass surgery</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Coronary_stent" title="Coronary stent">Coronary stent</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Angioplasty" title="Angioplasty">angioplasty</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vascular_surgery" title="Vascular surgery">Vascular surgery</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vein_stripping" title="Vein stripping">Vein stripping</a></li>
<li>Cosmetic procedures</li></ul>
<p>Cardiovascular procedures are more likely to be performed in an inpatient setting than in an ambulatory care setting; in the United States, only 28% of cardiovascular surgeries were performed in the ambulatory care setting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Open_Circulatroy.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Open_Circulatroy.gif/220px-Open_Circulatroy.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Open_Circulatroy.gif/330px-Open_Circulatroy.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Open_Circulatroy.gif/440px-Open_Circulatroy.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Open_Circulatroy.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The open circulatory system of the grasshopper – made up of a heart, vessels and hemolymph. The hemolymph is pumped through the heart, into the aorta, dispersed into the head and throughout the hemocoel, then back through the ostia in the heart and the process repeated.</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style>
<p>While humans, as well as other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">vertebrates</a>, have a closed blood circulatory system (meaning that the blood never leaves the network of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arteries" title="Arteries">arteries</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veins" title="Veins">veins</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capillaries" title="Capillaries">capillaries</a>), some <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrate</a> groups have an open circulatory system containing a heart but limited blood vessels. The most primitive, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploblastic" title="Diploblastic">diploblastic</a> animal <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">phyla</a> lack circulatory systems.
</p><p>An additional transport system, the lymphatic system, which is only found in animals with a closed blood circulation,  is an open system providing an accessory route for excess interstitial fluid to be returned to the blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherwood,_Lauralee_2011_401_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwood,_Lauralee_2011_401-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The blood vascular system first appeared probably in an ancestor of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triploblasts" title="Triploblasts">triploblasts</a> over 600 million years ago, overcoming the time-distance constraints of diffusion, while <a href="/wiki/Endothelium" title="Endothelium">endothelium</a> evolved in an ancestral <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> some 540–510 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Open_circulatory_system">Open circulatory system</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hemolymph" title="Hemolymph">Hemolymph</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, the open circulatory system is a system in which a fluid in a <a href="/wiki/Body_cavity" title="Body cavity">cavity</a> called the <b>hemocoel</b> bathes the organs directly with oxygen and nutrients, with there being no distinction between <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstitial_fluid" title="Interstitial fluid">interstitial fluid</a>; this combined fluid is called <a href="/wiki/Hemolymph" title="Hemolymph">hemolymph</a> or haemolymph.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Muscular movements by the animal during <a href="/wiki/Animal_locomotion" title="Animal locomotion">locomotion</a> can facilitate hemolymph movement, but diverting flow from one area to another is limited. When the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> relaxes, blood is drawn back toward the heart through open-ended pores (ostia).
</p><p>Hemolymph fills all of the interior hemocoel of the body and surrounds all <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>. Hemolymph is composed of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_chemistry" title="Inorganic chemistry">inorganic</a> <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> (mostly <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic compounds</a> (mostly <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>). The primary oxygen transporter molecule is <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">hemocyanin</a>.
</p><p>There are free-floating cells, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemocyte" title="Hemocyte">hemocytes</a>, within the hemolymph. They play a role in the arthropod <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pseudoceros_bifurcus_-_Blue_Pseudoceros_Flatworm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1145" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Pseudoceros_bifurcus_-_Blue_Pseudoceros_Flatworm.jpg/220px-Pseudoceros_bifurcus_-_Blue_Pseudoceros_Flatworm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Pseudoceros_bifurcus_-_Blue_Pseudoceros_Flatworm.jpg/330px-Pseudoceros_bifurcus_-_Blue_Pseudoceros_Flatworm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Pseudoceros_bifurcus_-_Blue_Pseudoceros_Flatworm.jpg/440px-Pseudoceros_bifurcus_-_Blue_Pseudoceros_Flatworm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pseudoceros_bifurcus_-_Blue_Pseudoceros_Flatworm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flatworms, such as this <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudoceros_bifurcus" title="Pseudoceros bifurcus">Pseudoceros bifurcus</a></i>, lack specialized circulatory organs.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Closed_circulatory_system">Closed circulatory system</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_chamber_heart.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Two_chamber_heart.svg/220px-Two_chamber_heart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Two_chamber_heart.svg/330px-Two_chamber_heart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Two_chamber_heart.svg/440px-Two_chamber_heart.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Two_chamber_heart.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two-chambered heart of a fish</div></div></div>
<p>The circulatory systems of all <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>, as well as of <a href="/wiki/Annelid" title="Annelid">annelids</a> (for example, <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a> (<a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Octopus" title="Octopus">octopuses</a> and relatives) always keep their circulating blood enclosed within heart chambers or blood vessels and are classified as <i>closed</i>, just as in humans. Still, the systems of <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> show various stages of the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> of the circulatory system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Closed systems permit blood to be directed to the organs that require it.
</p><p>In fish, the system has only one circuit, with the blood being pumped through the capillaries of the <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a> and on to the capillaries of the body tissues. This is known as <i>single cycle</i> circulation. The heart of fish is, therefore, only a single pump (consisting of two chambers).
</p><p>In amphibians and most reptiles, a double circulatory system is used, but the heart is not always completely separated into two pumps. Amphibians have a three-chambered heart.
</p><p>In reptiles, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ventricular_septum" title="Ventricular septum">ventricular septum</a> of the heart is incomplete and the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery" title="Pulmonary artery">pulmonary artery</a> is equipped with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sphincter_muscle" title="Sphincter muscle">sphincter muscle</a>. This allows a second possible route of blood flow. Instead of blood flowing through the pulmonary artery to the lungs, the sphincter may be contracted to divert this blood flow through the incomplete ventricular septum into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_ventricle" title="Left ventricle">left ventricle</a> and out through the <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a>. This means the blood flows from the capillaries to the heart and back to the capillaries instead of to the lungs. This process is useful to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ectothermic" title="Ectothermic">ectothermic</a> (cold-blooded) animals in the regulation of their body temperature.
</p><p>Mammals, birds and <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">crocodilians</a> show complete separation of the heart into two pumps, for a total of four heart chambers; it is thought that the four-chambered heart of birds and crocodilians evolved independently from that of mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Double circulatory systems permit blood to be repressurized after returning from the lungs, speeding up delivery of oxygen to tissues.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="No_circulatory_system">No circulatory system</span></h3>
<p>Circulatory systems are absent in some animals, including <a href="/wiki/Flatworm" title="Flatworm">flatworms</a>. Their <a href="/wiki/Body_cavity" title="Body cavity">body cavity</a> has no lining or enclosed fluid. Instead, a muscular <a href="/wiki/Pharynx" title="Pharynx">pharynx</a> leads to an extensively branched <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_system" title="Digestive system">digestive system</a> that facilitates direct <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffusion</a> of nutrients to all cells. The flatworm's dorso-ventrally flattened body shape also restricts the distance of any cell from the digestive system or the exterior of the organism. <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">Oxygen</a> can diffuse from the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> into the cells, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> can diffuse out. Consequently, every cell is able to obtain nutrients, water and oxygen without the need of a transport system.
</p><p>Some animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>, have more extensive branching from their <a href="/wiki/Gastrovascular_cavity" title="Gastrovascular cavity">gastrovascular cavity</a> (which functions as both a place of digestion and a form of circulation), this branching allows for bodily fluids to reach the outer layers, since the digestion begins in the inner layers.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit,_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam,_1543..JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="530" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG/220px-Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG/330px-Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG/440px-Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit%2C_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam%2C_1543..JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charta_ex_qva_figvram_parare_convenit,_illi_qvae_nervorvm_seriem_exprimit_appendendam,_1543..JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Human anatomical chart of blood vessels, with heart, lungs, liver and kidneys included. Other organs are numbered and arranged around it. Before cutting out the figures on this page, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesalius" title="Vesalius">Vesalius</a> suggests that readers glue the page onto parchment and gives instructions on how to assemble the pieces and paste the multilayered figure onto a base "muscle man" illustration. "Epitome", fol.14a. HMD Collection, WZ 240 V575dhZ 1543.</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest known writings on the circulatory system are found in the <a href="/wiki/Ebers_Papyrus" title="Ebers Papyrus">Ebers Papyrus</a> (16th century BCE), an <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_medicine" title="Ancient Egyptian medicine">ancient Egyptian medical papyrus</a> containing over 700 prescriptions and remedies, both physical and spiritual. In the <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a>, it acknowledges the connection of the heart to the arteries. The Egyptians thought air came in through the mouth and into the lungs and heart. From the heart, the air travelled to every member through the arteries. Although this concept of the circulatory system is only partially correct, it represents one of the earliest accounts of scientific thought.
</p><p>In the 6th century BCE, the knowledge of circulation of vital fluids through the body was known to the <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurvedic</a> physician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sushruta" title="Sushruta">Sushruta</a> in <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">ancient India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dwivedi&amp;Dwivedi07_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dwivedi&amp;Dwivedi07-29">[29]</a></sup> He also seems to have possessed knowledge of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arteries" title="Arteries">arteries</a>, described as 'channels' by Dwivedi &amp; Dwivedi (2007).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dwivedi&amp;Dwivedi07_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dwivedi&amp;Dwivedi07-29">[29]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Heart_valve" title="Heart valve">valves of the heart</a> were discovered by a physician of the <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocratean</a> school around the 4th century BCE. However, their function was not properly understood then. Because blood pools in the veins after death, arteries look empty. Ancient anatomists assumed they were filled with air and that they were for the transport of air.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_Medicine" title="Ancient Greek Medicine">Greek physician</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herophilus" title="Herophilus">Herophilus</a>, distinguished veins from arteries but thought that the <a href="/wiki/Pulse" title="Pulse">pulse</a> was a property of arteries themselves. Greek anatomist <a href="/wiki/Erasistratus" title="Erasistratus">Erasistratus</a> observed that arteries that were cut during life bleed. He ascribed the fact to the phenomenon that air escaping from an artery is replaced with blood that enters between veins and arteries by very small vessels. Thus he apparently postulated capillaries but with reversed flow of blood.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 2nd-century AD <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_Medicine" title="Ancient Greek Medicine">Greek</a> physician <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> knew that blood vessels carried blood and identified venous (dark red) and arterial (brighter and thinner) blood, each with distinct and separate functions. Growth and energy were derived from venous blood created in the liver from chyle, while arterial blood gave vitality by containing pneuma (air) and originated in the heart. Blood flowed from both creating organs to all parts of the body where it was consumed and there was no return of blood to the heart or liver. The heart did not pump blood around, the heart's motion sucked blood in during diastole and the blood moved by the pulsation of the arteries themselves.
</p><p>Galen believed that the arterial blood was created by venous blood passing from the left ventricle to the right by passing through 'pores' in the interventricular septum, air passed from the lungs via the pulmonary artery to the left side of the heart. As the arterial blood was created 'sooty' vapors were created and passed to the lungs also via the pulmonary artery to be exhaled.
</p><p>In 1025, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canon_of_Medicine" title="The Canon of Medicine">The Canon of Medicine</a></i> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Iranian_Medicine" title="Ancient Iranian Medicine">Persian physician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>, "erroneously accepted the Greek notion regarding the existence of a hole in the ventricular septum by which the blood traveled between the ventricles." Despite this, Avicenna "correctly wrote on the <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_cycle" title="Cardiac cycle">cardiac cycles</a> and valvular function", and "had a vision of blood circulation" in his <i>Treatise on Pulse</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag needs to be fact-checked with the cited source(s). (September 2010)">verification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> While also refining Galen's erroneous theory of the pulse, Avicenna provided the first correct explanation of pulsation: "Every beat of the pulse comprises two movements and two pauses. Thus, expansion : pause : contraction : pause. [...] The pulse is a movement in the heart and arteries ... which takes the form of alternate expansion and contraction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hajar_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hajar-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1242, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medicine_in_medieval_Islam" title="Medicine in medieval Islam">Arabian physician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Ibn al-Nafis</a> described the process of <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation">pulmonary circulation</a> in greater, more accurate detail than his predecessors, though he believed, as they did, in the notion of vital spirit (<a href="/wiki/Pneuma" title="Pneuma">pneuma</a>), which he believed was formed in the left ventricle. Ibn al-Nafis stated in his <i>Commentary on Anatomy in Avicenna's Canon</i>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>"...the blood from the right chamber of the heart must arrive at the left chamber but there is no direct pathway between them. The thick septum of the heart is not perforated and does not have visible pores as some people thought or invisible pores as Galen thought. The blood from the right chamber must flow through the vena arteriosa (<a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery" title="Pulmonary artery">pulmonary artery</a>) to the lungs, spread through its substances, be mingled there with air, pass through the arteria venosa (<a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_vein" title="Pulmonary vein">pulmonary vein</a>) to reach the left chamber of the heart and there form the vital spirit..."</p></blockquote>
<p>In addition, Ibn al-Nafis had an insight into what would become a larger theory of the <a href="/wiki/Capillary" title="Capillary">capillary</a> circulation. He stated that "there must be small communications or pores (<i>manafidh</i> in Arabic) between the pulmonary artery and vein," a prediction that preceded the discovery of the capillary system by more than 400 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Ibn al-Nafis' theory, however, was confined to blood transit in the lungs and did not extend to the entire body.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Servetus" title="Michael Servetus">Michael Servetus</a> was the first European to describe the function of pulmonary circulation, although his achievement was not widely recognized at the time, for a few reasons. He firstly described it in the "Manuscript of Paris"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> (near 1546), but this work was never published. And later he published this description, but in a theological treatise, <i>Christianismi Restitutio</i>, not in a book on medicine. Only three copies of the book survived but these remained hidden for decades, the rest were burned shortly after its publication in 1553 because of persecution of Servetus by religious authorities.
</p><p>Better known discovery of pulmonary circulation was by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesalius" title="Vesalius">Vesalius</a>'s successor at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">Padua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Realdo_Colombo" title="Realdo Colombo">Realdo Colombo</a>, in 1559.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Harvey_(1578-1657)_Venenbild.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="901" data-file-width="1293" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/220px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/330px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/440px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_Harvey_(1578-1657)_Venenbild.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Image of veins from <a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Exercitatio_Anatomica_de_Motu_Cordis_et_Sanguinis_in_Animalibus" title="Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus">Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus</a></i>, 1628</div></div></div>
<p>Finally, the English physician <a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a>, a pupil of <a href="/wiki/Hieronymus_Fabricius" title="Hieronymus Fabricius">Hieronymus Fabricius</a> (who had earlier described the valves of the veins without recognizing their function), performed a sequence of experiments and published his <i><a href="/wiki/Exercitatio_Anatomica_de_Motu_Cordis_et_Sanguinis_in_Animalibus" title="Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus">Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus</a></i> in 1628, which "demonstrated that there had to be a direct connection between the venous and arterial systems throughout the body, and not just the lungs. Most importantly, he argued that the beat of the heart produced a continuous circulation of blood through minute connections at the extremities of the body. This is a conceptual leap that was quite different from Ibn al-Nafis' refinement of the anatomy and bloodflow in the heart and lungs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> This work, with its essentially correct exposition, slowly convinced the medical world. However, Harvey was not able to identify the capillary system connecting arteries and veins; these were later discovered by <a href="/wiki/Marcello_Malpighi" title="Marcello Malpighi">Marcello Malpighi</a> in 1661.
</p><p>In 1956, <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Cournand" title="André Frédéric Cournand">André Frédéric Cournand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Werner_Forssmann" title="Werner Forssmann">Werner Forssmann</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dickinson_W._Richards" title="Dickinson W. Richards">Dickinson W. Richards</a> were awarded the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="List of Nobel laureates in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize</a> in Medicine "for their discoveries concerning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_catheterization" title="Heart catheterization">heart catheterization</a> and pathological changes in the circulatory system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
In his Nobel lecture, Forssmann credits Harvey as birthing cardiology with the publication of his book in 1628.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Diana_McSherry" title="Diana McSherry">Diana McSherry</a> developed computer-based systems to create images of the circulatory system and heart without the need for surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wayne_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wayne-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_diving_hazards_and_precautions" title="List of diving hazards and precautions">List of diving hazards and precautions</a></li>
<li>Environmental
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">Current</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delta-P_(hazard)" title="Delta-P (hazard)">Delta-P</a></li>

<li>Equipment
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Freeflow" title="Freeflow">Freeflow</a></li>
